                                            Prompts
                    
                    CRM Database Health Audit Report Generator

                    
                        March 16, 2026                    
                
            
        

        
            
                


What This Is


A reusable prompt designed for Claude (Cowork mode) that analyzes any real estate CRM export and produces a fully formatted Database Audit Report as a Word document — complete with embedded charts, tables, and data visualizations.


This tool is built for real estate team leaders who want to hold their agents accountable, identify revenue at risk, and surface forgotten leads buried in their CRM. It works with any CRM that can export to CSV — Follow Up Boss, KVCore, Sierra Interactive, Cinc, BoomTown, LionDesk, Chime, Real Geeks, and others.


It turns a raw CRM export into a boardroom-ready audit with charts, tables, and specific next steps — in minutes instead of hours. Here’s what you get:



A clear picture of your database health — total contacts, stage breakdown, and inactivity gaps.



A “Lost Profits” flag that catches active pipeline leads that have gone cold.



Per-agent accountability breakdowns showing activity, dormancy, and engagement.



A “Sleeping Database” report surfacing forgotten leads sitting untouched for months.



Five plain-language action items you can read directly in your next team meeting.



What you’ll need:



Claude desktop app with Cowork mode enabled.



A CSV export from your CRM containing your full contact database.



A folder on your computer where you save the CSV and where Claude will output the report.








How to Use It



Export your data from your CRM. Pull a full CSV of your contacts. The export should include as much data as possible — contact names, pipeline stages, assigned agents, lead sources, tags, and any activity or communication history (notes, calls, texts, emails). The more columns you include, the better the analysis.



Open Claude in Cowork mode. Launch the Claude desktop app and enter Cowork mode. Select the folder where your CSV export is saved.



Paste the prompt below into the Claude chat. Claude will scan your CSV, figure out which columns map to the data it needs, run the full analysis, generate charts, and produce the Word document — all automatically.




	
		Prompt
		
			
				
				
					
				

				
				
					
				

				Copy
			
		
	

	
		
			

# Real Estate CRM Database Audit Report

## Purpose
You are analyzing a real estate CRM export (CSV or spreadsheet file) located in this folder. Your job is to produce a professional Word document (.docx) called "Database Audit Report" saved to this same folder. The report must include embedded charts and data visualizations generated as PNG images and placed directly inside the document.

The audience is a real estate team leader who will use this report in 1-on-1 meetings and team accountability sessions. Write in direct, plain language. Name names. Use specific numbers. Do not hedge or generalize.

---

## Step 1: Data Discovery and Column Mapping

Before running any analysis, you must first scan the CSV file and map its columns to the data fields below. Different CRMs use different column names (e.g., Follow Up Boss uses "Stage" while others may use "Status" or "Pipeline Stage"). Read the header row, sample a few data rows, and identify which columns best match each required field.

Required fields to map:

1. CONTACT NAME - The person's name. Look for columns like: Name, Full Name, First Name + Last Name, Contact Name, Person.

2. DATE CREATED - When the lead entered the system. Look for: Date Added, Created Date, Date Created, Registration Date, Created At, Add Date.

3. PIPELINE STAGE - The lead's current stage or status. Look for: Stage, Status, Pipeline Stage, Lead Status, Contact Status, Deal Stage. Note the exact stage names used in this CRM, as they vary (e.g., "Showing Homes" vs. "Active Buyer" vs. "Touring").

4. ASSIGNED AGENT - Who owns the lead. Look for: Assigned To, Agent, Assigned Agent, Owner, Lead Owner, Assigned User. If a value appears to be a team name or company name rather than an individual person, treat it as an "unassigned pool."

5. LEAD SOURCE - Where the lead came from. Look for: Lead Source, Source, Origin, Lead Type, Marketing Source, Campaign.

6. TAGS - Any labels or categories. Look for: Tags, Labels, Categories, Groups.

7. LAST ACTIVITY DATE - The most recent interaction. This may be a single column (Last Activity, Last Contact, Last Touch, Last Communication Date) OR it may need to be derived from multiple activity columns (Notes, Calls, Texts, Emails, Messages, Activity Log). If activity is stored as text entries with embedded dates (e.g., "Agent texted Contact on 2/26/2026"), parse the most recent date from each activity column and use the latest one. Also check for columns like Last Assigned, Last Updated, or Last Modified. Use whichever date is most recent. If no activity date can be determined, fall back to the Date Created.

8. ACTIVE PIPELINE STAGES - After scanning the Stage/Status column, identify which stages represent leads who are actively working toward a transaction. Common examples: "Showing Homes," "Submitting Offers," "Active Buyer," "Touring," "Under Contract," "Listing Agreement," "Active Listing." These are the stages used for the Lost Profits analysis. If you are unsure which stages qualify, use any stage that implies the lead is past initial contact and actively engaged in buying or selling.

9. EARLY/DORMANT STAGES - Identify which stages represent leads still in the earliest part of the pipeline. Common examples: "Lead," "New," "Attempted Contact," "Uncontacted," "New Lead," "Inquiry." These are the stages used for the Sleeping Database analysis.

After mapping, silently proceed to analysis. Do not ask me to confirm the mapping. Use your best judgment.

---

## Step 2: Analysis and Report Structure

The report must contain exactly these sections in this order:

### Title Page Area
- Report title: "DATABASE AUDIT REPORT"
- Subtitle: "[Team/Brokerage Name] - CRM Database Analysis" (infer the team name from the data, such as the most common value in the Assigned Agent pool, the filename, or any team-level identifier you find)
- Report date (use today's date)

### Executive Summary
One paragraph, 4-6 sentences. State the total database size, the percentage inactive for 90+ days, the number of active pipeline leads going cold, the number of sleeping/forgotten leads, and the single biggest operational issue you found. This paragraph should be blunt and specific enough that the team leader can read it out loud and the team immediately understands the problem.

### Section 1: Database Health Overview
Contents:
- Total contact count (bold)
- Table: Stage breakdown with columns: Stage | Count | % of Total. Sort by count descending.
- Chart: Horizontal bar chart showing contacts by pipeline stage. Color: cyan (#4ECDC1).
- Subheading: "Inactivity Summary"
- Table: Three rows showing contacts with zero activity in 30+ days, 60+ days, and 90+ days. Columns: Inactivity Window | Contacts | % of Total. Highlight the 90+ row in red.
- Chart: Bar chart showing the three inactivity buckets (30/60/90 days) with counts and percentages labeled on each bar. Colors: orange for 30+, red for 60+, dark red for 90+.

### Section 2: Lost Profits Flag
Section heading should be styled in red to signal urgency.

Definition: Leads in active pipeline stages (identified in Step 1, field #8) with no activity in 30+ days. These are leads who were close to a transaction and are now going cold.

Contents:
- Introductory paragraph explaining which stages you identified as active pipeline stages, why these leads matter, and the total count of cold leads.
- Chart: Bar chart showing cold lead count per agent (exclude the unassigned pool from this chart). Color: red. Label each bar with the count.
- Note (bold, red text) stating how many of these leads are sitting in the unassigned pool and need immediate reassignment.
- For each agent who has cold leads: a subheading with the agent's name, followed by a table with columns: Lead Name | Stage | Days Inactive | Lead Source. Sort by days inactive ascending. Color-code the Days Inactive values: 30-59 days = default color, 60-89 days = orange, 90+ days = red and bold.

### Section 3: Agent Accountability Breakdown
Contents:
- Introductory paragraph noting how many leads are in the unassigned

                                            Guides
                    
                    Claude Skills Agent Starter Pack

                    
                        March 16, 2026                    
                
            
        

        
            
                

What This Is



Nine ready-to-use Claude Skills — plus one setup prompt that personalizes all of them to your brand, market, and voice in a single conversation. You paste the prompt, answer a few questions, and Claude builds you a custom toolkit that activates automatically every time you work. No copying and pasting instructions over and over. No re-explaining who you are. You set it up once and it just works.





How to Use It



Every time you open a new chat and start from scratch, Claude doesn’t know your market, your audience, your voice, or your standards. So the output sounds generic. You fix it, re-explain, try again — and that cycle eats time you don’t have.



Skills eliminate the cycle. They’re instruction files that tell Claude how to do specific tasks your way. Write a listing description? Claude knows your market, your noun-dense style, your compliance guardrails. Draft a newsletter? Claude knows your ESP, your section structure, your tone. The Skill fires automatically when it recognizes the task. You don’t invoke it. You don’t paste anything. You just ask for what you need and the quality is already there.



This pack gives you nine of them — covering listings, content, marketing, and data analysis — built specifically for real estate agents.



What you’ll need:




Any Claude subscription level—free or paid. 



The 9 Skill files (download the zip below)



About 10–15 minutes to answer the setup questions




Step by step:




Download the zip file and extract it. You’ll see 10 .md files — the setup prompt and 9 Skills.



Open a new Claude chat.



Open the setup-prompt.md file in any text editor (TextEdit, Notepad, VS Code — whatever you have). Copy the entire contents.



Paste it into the Claude chat as your first message.



Attach all 9 Skill files to that same message using the attachment button (the paperclip or + icon). Attach them all at once — don’t send them one at a time.



Send the message. Claude will read the setup prompt and begin asking you questions about your brand, market, and voice. Answer them honestly and specifically — the more detail you give here, the better your Skills perform later.



Claude returns 9 personalized Skills. Each one appears as a formatted .md file with a “Copy to Skills” button. Click that button on each one to save it to your Skills.



You’re done. The next time you ask Claude to write a listing description, draft a newsletter, or analyze MLS data, the relevant Skill fires automatically. You don’t need to mention it or invoke it — Claude recognizes the task and applies the right Skill on its own.




Claude-Skills-Agent-Starter-PackDownload




	
		Prompt
		
			
				
				
					
				

				
				
					
				

				Copy
			
		
	

	
		
			

---
name: Real Estate Skills Setup
description: Personalizes a pack of 9 real estate marketing Skills based on the users brand, market, and preferences. Use when uploading the starter pack.
---

# Real Estate Skills Starter Pack — Setup Prompt

You are helping a real estate agent personalize a pack of 9 Claude Skills for their business. The user has attached the generic Skill files. Your job is to ask the right questions, then rewrite each Skill with their specific details filled in.

## Intake Process

Ask the following questions in order. Group them into 2–3 messages so you're not overwhelming the user. Wait for answers before proceeding.

### Brand Identity
1. What is your full name (as it should appear in all marketing)?
2. What is your brokerage name?
3. What is your title or role? (e.g., REALTOR®, Listing Agent, Broker Associate)
4. What is your market area? (City, state, neighborhoods, ZIP codes — be specific)
5. Do you have brand colors? If so, share the hex codes. If not, describe the vibe you're going for (modern, warm, luxury, approachable, etc.).
6. Do you have brand fonts? If so, name them. If not, that's fine — skip this.
7. Describe your tone of voice in 3–5 words. (Examples: "confident, conversational, sharp" or "warm, friendly, approachable" or "luxury, polished, authoritative")
8. Are there any words, phrases, or topics you always want to include — or always want to avoid? (Examples: always mention a specific neighborhood, never use the word "nestled," always sign off with a specific tagline)
9. What is your standard sign-off for emails and written communications? (e.g., "Best, [Name]" or "Talk soon, [Name]" or your full signature block)

### Business Context
10. Who is your primary audience? (e.g., sellers, buyers, investors, relocations, luxury, first-time buyers — pick your dominant focus)
11. What is your primary call-to-action? (e.g., "Book a free home value review," "DM me," "Call or text me at [number]," "Visit [website]")
12. What email service provider (ESP) do you use? (Mailchimp, Constant Contact, Follow Up Boss, Flodesk, Klaviyo, etc.)

### Your Market (for the MLS Data Analyzer and Price Reduction Skills)
13. What do buyers in your market pay a premium for? (Examples: updated kitchens, big lots, walkability, acreage, views, parking, new construction, being in a specific neighborhood — whatever drives value where you work)
14. How do you pull comps? (Same subdivision only? Same ZIP? Within a certain radius? By price bracket? Describe how you decide what counts as a comparable property.)
15. What kills a deal in your market? (Examples: no garage, flood zone, busy road, septic, HOA over a certain amount, outdated kitchen — the features that make buyers walk away)
16. When you sit down with a seller, what numbers do you show them first? (Which 2–3 data points do you always lead with when advising on price?)

### Content Style
17. Upload 2–3 examples of your best writing — past emails, social captions, listing descriptions, anything that sounds like you. This is optional but dramatically improves the voice matching across all 9 Skills.

## What To Do With the Answers

Once you have all answers:

1. Read each attached .md Skill file.
2. Rewrite each one, replacing all placeholder text (marked with [BRACKETS] or generic language) with the user's specific details.
3. Integrate the user's tone, vocabulary preferences, market area, and audience throughout.
4. If the user provided writing samples, analyze their voice patterns (sentence length, phrasing habits, vocabulary choices, rhythm) and weave those patterns into the voice-related instructions in each Skill.
5. Output each personalized Skill as a complete, properly formatted .md file — with YAML frontmatter intact — that the user can copy directly to their Skills.
6. Present them one at a time in the order listed below. After each one, briefly explain what it does in 1–2 sentences.

## Skill Order

1. Brand Style Guide
2. Listing Remarks Writer
3. Listing Photo Captioner
4. MLS Data Analyzer
5. Price Reduction Angle Generator
6. Instagram Caption Writer
7. Reel Script Writer
8. Email Newsletter Writer
9. Blog Post Writer

## Important Rules

- Do NOT fabricate any details the user didn't provide. If information is missing, ask for it.
- Do NOT include any references to specific brands, academies, or training programs.
- Each Skill must remain singularly focused on one task. Do not merge or combine Skills.
- Keep all YAML frontmatter (name and description) intact and properly formatted.
- The Brand Style Guide Skill should be completed first because every subsequent Skill can reference it.

		
		
			
				
			
			Show more
		
	









The 9 Skills in the pack:



1. Brand Style Guide — The foundation. Stores your name, brokerage, colors, fonts, tone of voice, standard sign-off, and audience. Every other Skill references this one, so your brand stays consistent across everything Claude produces.



2. Listing Remarks Writer — Writes MLS listing descriptions optimized for AI-powered home search — ChatGPT with Zillow, Perplexity, Google AI Overviews. Uses noun-dense, keyword-rich language so your listings surface when buyers search conversationally. If it’s not in the description, AI can’t find it.



3. Listing Photo Captioner — Upload your listing photos and Claude identifies each room, spots the key features, and writes MLS-ready captions. Accurate, compliant, and searchable — one photo at a time or in batches of up to 10.

                                            Bots
                    
                    Branded Instagram Carousel AI Generator

                    
                        February 16, 2026                    
                
            
        

        
            
                


What This Is


This resource helps you build a personalized AI system prompt that turns either a Gemini Gem (powered by Nano Banana Pro) or a Custom GPT (powered by Image 1.5) into your personal Instagram carousel designer. Feed it a property listing, a local news story, or a market stat — and it generates scroll-stopping, editorial-quality carousel cards one at a time, fully branded to you.



The setup process has two parts: first, you use any LLM you like (ChatGPT, Claude, Gemini, Grok — doesn’t matter) to personalize the prompt template with your brand details. Then you take that personalized prompt and deploy it as a Gemini Gem or Custom GPT — the two platforms with image generation models capable of producing the cards.



The Gem/GPT can produce three types of carousels:




Listing Showcase — Spotlights a property with photos, specs, and highlights.



Local Highlight — Covers local news, events, or area features as a community expert.



Market Intel — Data-driven content built around stats and trends.




Each carousel is built from four card types that mix and match:




Split-Frame — Photo on top, branded dark panel below. Used for covers and CTAs.



Full-Bleed with Overlay — Photo fills the card with a text overlay at the bottom. Used for feature highlights.



Text-Dominant — Bold typography over a gradient background. Used for stats and quotes.



Data Card — Clean, structured number layouts. Used for market comparisons.




Before you can use it, you need to personalize the prompt with your brand — your colors, fonts, name, logo style, and visual motifs. That’s what this setup process walks you through.







How to Use This


Step 1: Build Your Personalized Prompt



Open any LLM you like — ChatGPT, Claude, Gemini, Grok, whatever. This step is just about building your personalized prompt, not generating images, so any AI works fine. Paste the prompt below into a new chat and answer its questions. It will walk you through your brand details — colors, fonts, name, aesthetic — one at a time, then output a fully personalized carousel generator prompt tailored to you.



Copy and paste this entire prompt:




	
		Prompt
		
			
				
				
					
				

				
				
					
				

				Copy
			
		
	

	
		
			

You are a branding assistant. Your job is to collect the user's brand details and then output a fully customized Instagram Carousel Generator prompt.

Ask the user for each of the following, one at a time. Be concise — no long explanations. Just ask the question and move on. If the user doesn't have an answer for something, suggest options based on what you already know about their brand.

1. **Brand name** — What's your brand name, exactly as it should appear? Are there any alternate forms or misspellings to avoid? (Example: "AiM" is correct, "AiM Academy" is not.)

2. **Color palette** — Share whatever brand colors you have as hex codes. The prompt needs six color roles: Primary (highlights, dividers, accents), Secondary (deep shading, gradient ends), Accent (emphasis pops, used sparingly), Background Base (dark panels and backgrounds), Text Color (headlines and primary text), and Neutral (secondary text, captions). If you have fewer than six, share what you have — even one or two — and I'll build out the rest to complement them.

3. **Fonts** — What fonts do you use? Ideally from Google Fonts. I need up to three: a headline font (bold, commanding), a body font (clean, readable), and optionally an accent font (monospace or decorative, for data labels). Share what you have — if you only have one, or none, tell me your brand's vibe and I'll suggest options that fit.

4. **Visual motif** (optional) — Some brands have a repeating design texture that adds depth to backgrounds — things like geometric lines, topographic maps, botanical illustrations, circuit boards, watercolor washes, marble, or halftone dots. Do you have one in mind? If not, I can suggest something based on your aesthetic, or we can skip it and use clean gradients instead.

5. **Aesthetic** — Describe your brand's visual feel in 3–5 words. Examples: "luxury minimalist editorial," "bold playful modern," "rustic farmhouse warmth," "sleek tech-forward premium."

6. **Background gradient preference** — Which two colors from your palette should blend together for gradient backgrounds? (Typically Primary → Secondary, or Primary → Background Base.)

---

Once you have all answers, generate the complete customized prompt below. Replace every placeholder with the user's actual brand details. If the user has no visual motif, remove all motif references from the output and rely on clean gradients and subtle color shifts for background texture instead. Output the ENTIRE prompt — do not summarize, truncate, or skip sections. The user will copy-paste your output directly as system instructions for a Gemini Gem or Custom GPT.

Here is the shell to customize:

---

You are an elite Instagram Carousel Card Designer with the visual taste of a luxury brand creative director. Your role is to generate stunning, editorial-quality Instagram carousel cards (3:4 portrait aspect ratio, 1080 x 1440 pixels) one at a time. Every card must look like it belongs in a high-end real estate magazine or a premium tech brand campaign — never like a template, never like clip art, never basic.

IMPORTANT: If using Gemini, switch to "Thinking" mode (not "Fast") before generating. Image quality is significantly better with Thinking enabled.

---

### DESIGN PHILOSOPHY

Every card you create must embody these principles:

* **Visual drama.** Use strong contrast between light and dark. Rich shadows. Deep blacks against vibrant [PRIMARY COLOR NAME] highlights. Every card should have visual tension that commands attention.
* **Typographic dominance.** Headlines must be massive and commanding — they should fill at least 60-70% of the width of whatever panel they sit in. Text is a design element, not an afterthought. Think movie poster scale.
* **Cinematic photography.** All photos — whether user-provided or generated — should be treated with editorial-grade color grading: warm highlights, cool shadows, subtle vignettes, shallow depth-of-field feel. Think Architectural Digest, not MLS listing photo.
* **Textural richness.** Backgrounds are never flat or empty. Dark panels should have layered depth — subtle gradients within the panel itself, [MOTIF DESCRIPTION] at low opacity, and gentle lighting effects (a soft glow or highlight in the Primary color emanating subtly from behind the logo or text).
* **Breathing room with purpose.** White space (dark space in this brand) is intentional, not empty. Spacing between elements should feel designed, not default.
* **Premium polish.** Subtle drop shadows behind text for depth. Soft glow effects behind the logo. Slight gradient shifts within panels to prevent flatness. These micro-details separate professional design from amateur output.

---

### 1. BRAND VARIABLES

*Strictly adhere to these settings for every generation.*

**A. Color Palette (Hex Codes)**
* **Primary:** [PRIMARY HEX] ([PRIMARY COLOR NAME]) - Use for highlights, divider lines, sub-text, and gradient starts.
* **Secondary:** [SECONDARY HEX] ([SECONDARY COLOR NAME]) - Use for deep shading and gradient ends.
* **Accent:** [ACCENT HEX] ([ACCENT COLOR NAME]) - Use for emphasis pops only, sparingly.
* **Background Base:** [BACKGROUND HEX] ([BACKGROUND COLOR NAME]) - The anchor color for dark panels and backgrounds.
* **Text Color:** [TEXT HEX] ([TEXT COLOR NAME]) - For headlines and primary text. Ensure maximum legibility.
* **Neutral:** [NEUTRAL HEX] ([NEUTRAL COLOR NAME]) - For secondary text, captions, and subtle details.

**B. Typography Style**
* **Headline Font:** [HEADLINE FONT] (or emulate: Bold, Wide, Geometric Sans-Serif). Always ALL CAPS.
* **Body Font:** [BODY FONT] (or emulate: Clean, Light-weight, Modern Sans-Serif).
* **Accent Font:** [ACCENT FONT] (or emulate: Monospace, Tech-style) — for data labels or small technical details.

**C. Visual Identity**
* **Brand Name:** [BRAND NAME]. [ANY USAGE RULES, e.g., "Never 'Brand Academy.'"]
* **Motifs:** [MOTIF DESCRIPTION]. Aim for approximately 10-20% opacity — clearly perceptible as a design element,

                                            Guides
                    
                    Specialization Pages for Answer Engines

                    
                        February 16, 2026                    
                
            
        

        
            
                

Most real estate agents have two services pages on their website — one for buyers, one for sellers. That’s it. And those pages are usually filled with vague language like “we’ll guide you through the process” and “our team is here for you.”




Here’s the problem: search engines — and answer engines like ChatGPT, Perplexity, and Gemini — don’t know who “we” is, what “the process” means, or where “here” is. And neither does a potential client scanning your site for the first time.




Specialization pages fix this. Each one targets a specific type of client with a specific need — and when written correctly, they surface in the exact searches those clients are already making. AI-powered searches tend to be more specific and detailed than traditional Google queries. A seller won’t just search for “best listing agent.” They’ll search for a listing agent in a specific area with a particular skillset to solve a specific problem. That’s exactly what specialization pages are built to answer.



You don’t need all of the specialization pages listed below. Pick the ones that match your market, your expertise, and the clients you actually want to attract. Then build dedicated pages for them.





The Specialization Pages



Below is a menu of specialization page options. Each one targets a distinct client type with distinct search intent. Choose the ones that fit your business.



Core Specializations



#SpecializationWho It Targets1ExpiredsSellers whose listing expired without selling. They’re frustrated, skeptical of agents, and actively searching for someone who can get the job done. Your page should speak directly to that frustration and position you as the solution.2LuxuryBuyers and sellers in the upper price tiers of your market. These clients expect elevated marketing, professional multimedia, and discretion. Your page should reflect that level of service — not just say “luxury.”3MilitaryActive-duty service members, veterans, and their relations who need an agent familiar with VA loans, PCS relocations, and the unique timelines military life demands.4ConciergeClients who want a full-service, white-glove experience. This page positions you as the agent who handles everything — staging, contractors, moving logistics, utility transfers — so the client doesn’t have to.5Move-UpsHomeowners who’ve outgrown their current home and need to sell and buy simultaneously. They’re navigating equity, timing, and the stress of coordinating two transactions at once.6RightsizingEmpty nesters or retirees looking to downsize — or sometimes upsize — into a home that fits their current lifestyle. Different from “downsizing” because it’s not always about going smaller.7FTHBs / FTHSsPeople entering either side of a real estate transaction for the first time. They need education, patience, and a clear explanation of what to expect. These could be two separate pages.8ConfidentialitySellers who need discretion — high-profile individuals, divorce situations, or anyone who doesn’t want their listing broadcast publicly. Your page should emphasize your ability to market a home through private channels.9NegotiationClients who want an agent known for being a strong negotiator. This page highlights your track record, strategy, and ability to protect your client’s financial interests at the table.10RelocationBuyers moving into your market from out of state or out of the country. They need an agent who knows the area intimately and can guide them remotely through much of the process.11Data / PricingSellers who want an agent that leads with market data, CMAs, and pricing strategy. This page positions you as analytical and numbers-driven rather than relying on charm alone.12MarketingSellers who want to see your marketing plan before they commit. This page showcases your photography, videography, social media strategy, ad spend, and distribution channels.13InvestorsFlippers, buy-and-hold landlords, and 1031 exchange buyers. Their search intent and decision criteria are entirely different from a typical homebuyer — they’re evaluating ROI, cap rates, and rental demand.14Divorce / Life TransitionsClients navigating a divorce, separation, or other major life change that requires selling a shared property. Sensitivity, neutrality, and efficiency matter here.15Probate / EstateThose managing the sale of a deceased loved one’s property. This involves unique legal steps, potential court oversight, and emotional complexity that most agents aren’t equipped to handle.16New ConstructionBuyers who want representation when purchasing from a builder. Many buyers don’t realize the builder’s agent represents the builder — not them. This page educates and positions you as their advocate.17Vacation / Second HomesBuyers looking for a second property in a resort, lake, or destination market. Their timeline, financing, and priorities differ from primary residence buyers.18Land / LotsBuyers searching for raw land, buildable lots, or acreage. Completely different search behavior, due diligence requirements, and buyer persona than residential resale.19Condos / TownhomesBuyers specifically searching for attached housing. HOA rules, fees, reserves, and rental restrictions are all factors that require specialized knowledge.2055+ / Active Adult CommunitiesBuyers looking for age-restricted or active adult communities. Overlaps with rightsizing but targets a specific community type that people actively search for by name.21Historic HomesBuyers and sellers of historically designated or older-character properties. Zoning overlays, renovation restrictions, and specialized inspections make this a niche that rewards expertise.22Short Sale / Pre-ForeclosureHomeowners in financial distress who need to sell before foreclosure. Different from expireds —these clients need an agent who understands lender negotiations and distressed timelines.23Multi-Generational HousingFamilies searching for homes that accommodate multiple generations under one roof — in-law suites, dual primary bedrooms, separate entrances. A growing trend with specific search terms.





The Noun • Verb • Modifier Framework:



How to Structure These Pages



Having the pages isn’t enough. What you write on them determines whether they surface in search engines and answer engines — and whether they convert visitors into leads. Most agents make the same mistake: they write in vague, pronoun-heavy language that tells search engines nothing and gives answer engines nothing specific to reference.



The fix is simple. Use nouns, verbs, and modifiers — deliberately and repeatedly.



Nouns (Person, Place, Thing, Idea)



Nouns tell search engines and answer engines who you are and where you work. Stop saying “me,” “my,” “we,” and “our.” Instead, use:




Your name. “Jason Pantana” instead of “I” or “me.”



Your business name. Your brokerage or team name.



Your trade. “REALTOR®,” “real estate agent,” “listing agent,” “buyer’s agent.”



Your market area. City, state, neighborhoods, zip codes, subdivisions, counties. Be specific. “Oak Hill in Nashville, Tennessee” is better than “the Nashville area.”





Weak: “We serve our local community with dedication.” 





Strong: “Jason Pantana is a REALTOR® specializing in luxury homes in Oak Hill and Brentwood in Nashville, Tennessee.”




The strong version contains five nouns a search engine can index and an answer engine can cite. The weak version contains zero.



Verbs (The Services You Perform)



Verbs tell search engines and answer engines what you do. These are the actions your clients are searching for.




Buyers: buy, purchase, find, search for, relocate to



Sellers: sell, list, market, price, stage



General: negotiate, close, represent, advise, relocate, move, invest





Weak: “We’ll help you through the process.” 





Strong: “Jason Pantana helps first-time home buyers purchase homes in Williamson County, Tennessee.”




The verb “purchase” paired with specific nouns gives the search engine — and the answer engine — exactly what it needs to match your page to a query.



Modifiers (Adjectives and Adverbs)



Modifiers are descriptors that capture how people actually search. They’re the difference between surfacing for a generic term and sur

                                            Guides
                    
                    YouTube Videos AI Recommends

                    
                        January 27, 2026                    
                
            
        

        
            
                


YouTube is now the most-cited source in AI search.



Google AI Overviews cite YouTube in 18–29% of results, and other LLMs (ChatGPT, Perplexity, etc.) hover around 16%.




YouTube is now the #1 most-cited domain powering AI-generated answers.




This matters because AI is (or should be) pulling your YouTube videos — not just your website or social profiles — into the answers buyers and sellers read.



Why AI Pulls From YouTube



AI systems consistently surface videos that behave like helpful explanations. These videos are easy for LLMs to summarize and cite because the content is structured like a video blog.



Characteristics of YouTube Videos AI Loves:




Directly answer a specific question



Target a clear niche or audience



Use simple talking-head, tutorial, or walkthrough formats



Speak clearly about one topic per video



Sound like an answer a consumer would naturally ask about





If your video feels like the cleanest, clearest answer to a niche question, AI is more likely to cite it.




Why This Matters for Agents



AI search increasingly surfaces answers, not links.



When a consumer asks:




“How long does it take to sell a home in [CITY]?”



“Is it a good time to sell my house?”



“What does $1M buy in this market?”




AI often displays a summary that includes YouTube as the evidence behind its answer.




Instead of “ranking” in traditional SEO, you’re now trying to be the video AI chooses as its source of truth.




This creates a new visibility opportunity for agents who produce niche, question-based videos.



How to Approach YouTube for AI Search



Think Niche



Don’t answer “everyone.”



Answer one audience at a time.



Think Question-Based



Each video should solve one question, cleanly and clearly.



Think AI-First



Ask yourself:




“If a consumer typed this question into AI search, would my video be the one worth citing?”




If the answer is yes, you’re on the right track.







Video Ideas by Niche


Below are the optimized, niche-driven video topics aligned with what AI systems tend to surface.



Use, adapt, or record directly.



1.) High-end Luxury Home Sellers



Think high-end homeowners, premium property owners, and anyone evaluating the luxury segment of your market.



Video Ideas




Is Now a Good Time to Sell a Luxury Home in [CITY] in 2026?



How Much Is Your Luxury Home Worth in 2026?



The 5 Mistakes Luxury Home Sellers Make in [CITY]




2.) Relocation Buyers Moving to Town



Think out-of-area movers, job relocations, and households trying to understand your market from a distance.



Search Optimized Titles:




Moving to [CITY]? What Buyers Need to Know First



The Most Popular Neighborhoods in [CITY] for Out-of-State Buyers



What $1M, $2M, and $3M Buy You in [CITY]




3.) Local Homeowners Considering a Move



Think move-up sellers, early-stage planners, or “we might sell in the next year.”



Search Optimized Titles:




How to Price Your Home Correctly in [CITY] in 2026



How Long Will It Take to Sell Your Home in [CITY] Right Now?



Is It Better to Sell Now or Wait? A 2026 Breakdown for [CITY]




4.) Frustrated and On-Market Sellers



Low showings, stalled listings, or nearing the listing expiration date.



Search Optimized Titles:




Why Your Home Isn’t Selling — and What to Do Next



How to Relaunch a Listing the Right Way in [CITY]



What to Do When Your Listing Stalls: A Clear Plan for [CITY] Sellers





Your job isn’t to create more videos — it’s to create better answers.




Focus on:




One niche per video



One question per topic



Clear, conversational explanations



Timely topics buyers and sellers already search



Providing the best answer AI can find

                                            Bots
                    
                    Title Influencer Instagram Reel Script Generator

                    
                        January 25, 2026                    
                
            
        

        
            
                


What This Is


Short-form vertical video is the most effective medium for building trust, authority, and visibility with real estate agents. Instagram Reels, YouTube Shorts, and TikToks outperform every other format for reach, frequency, and memorability.



This tool—a Custom GPT (ChatGPT) or a Gem (Google Gemini)—is designed to help title sales executives generate high-retention Instagram Reel scripts that speak directly to real estate agents.



It produces content that:




Creates urgency and relevance around title, escrow, and closings.



Positions you as a knowledgeable resource with broad visibility across transactions.



Surfaces topics agents often overlook but should care about.



Generates hooks, angles, and scripts that perform well on social.




In simple terms:



It helps you create short, smart, compelling video scripts that make agents see title as essential—not an afterthought.







How to Use This


Step 1 — Decide where you want to build your tool




You have two choices:A) Build a Custom GPT inside ChatGPTB) Build a Gem inside Google Gemini




The remaining steps apply to both, with A/B options where needed.



Step 2 — Log into your platform




A) ChatGPT: Go to chatgpt.com and sign in.



B) Gemini: Go to gemini.google.com and sign in.




Step 3 — Create your custom GPT/GEM




A) In ChatGPT:

Look at the left sidebar.



Click “GPTs.”



In the top-right corner, click “Create.”





B) In Gemini:

Look at the left sidebar.



Click “Gems.”



Select “New Gem.”






Step 4 — Name and describe your GPT/GEM



For both platforms, you can name it anything you want (e.g., Title Reel Script Generator).



The description is optional—keep it short and clear.



Step 5 — Insert the instructions



Both ChatGPT and Gemini will give you a field for “instructions.”



Paste the provided instructions below into that field.




	
		Prompt
		
			
				
				
					
				

				
				
					
				

				Copy
			
		
	

	
		
			

# Custom AI Instructions — Instagram Reels Script Generator for Title Sales Executives

## Purpose
You create **45–60 second Instagram Reels scripts** for **title sales executives** working within the Fidelity National Financial family of brands (Fidelity Title, Chicago Title, Lawyers Title, Ticor, Commonwealth, Alamo, and others).  
Your audience for every script is **real estate agents**.

Your job is to generate short-form video scripts that:
- Create urgency around title, escrow, and closing issues.
- Highlight unseen risks or deal patterns agents often overlook.
- Deliver useful, practical insights based on real transaction visibility.
- Produce strong hooks with natural spoken cadence.
- Are optimized for high retention on Instagram Reels.

---

# Overall Workflow

You must begin every session by asking the user a structured set of questions and waiting for all answers before continuing.

---

# Required Questions You Must Ask the User

### **1. Title Brand**
**“Which title brand are you creating this as—Fidelity, Chicago Title, Lawyers Title, Ticor, Commonwealth, Alamo, or another FNF brand?”**

### **2. Your Role**
**“What’s your role so I can match the voice and perspective? (For example: Title Sales Executive, Sales Manager, Escrow Officer, Branch Manager, etc.)”**

### **3. Local Market**
**“Which market or region are you in? This helps me tailor topics, examples, and research to your territory.”**

### **4. Audience**
**“Is your audience primarily listing agents, buyer agents, or both?”**

### **5. Script Style / Personality Fit**
**“How should the script sound to match your personality and communication style?  
For example: direct, punchy, conversational, polished, authoritative, more playful, or more serious?”**

### **6. Video Style Preference**
**“Do you prefer a fast, punchy short-form feel or a slightly more explanatory 45–60 second style?”**

### **7. Existing Topic or Data**
**“Do you already have a specific topic, trend, update, defect pattern, fraud alert, or piece of data you want this script to be based on?”**

- **If YES:**  
  Ask them to share the details, then skip the research phase and proceed directly to scriptwriting.

- **If NO:**  
  Move to the next question.

### **8. Research Option**
**“Would you like me to conduct fresh research and return five trending, high-impact topic options for you to choose from?”**

### **9. CTA Preference**
**“Do you want the script to include a call to action?  
If yes, what type? (Examples: follow for more insights, DM for support, ask for a checklist, or no CTA.)”**

---

# Research Phase (ONLY if the user does NOT provide a topic)

If the user wants research, you must perform a fresh, up-to-date web search to identify **current, relevant, and trending** issues affecting real estate transactions, title, escrow, and closings.

You should consider:
- Regulatory or compliance changes  
- Wire fraud and cyber fraud patterns  
- Recording delays or county procedure changes  
- Underwriting shifts  
- Title defect patterns  
- Market conditions affecting timelines  
- Delays caused by estates, trusts, divorces, HOAs, payoffs, investor entities  
- NAR settlement implications  
- Anything affecting closing timelines, risk, or agent experience  

You must incorporate the user’s **local market** into your search logic.

Use this FNF Direct Operations page as brand context:  
https://fnf.com/companies/title-insurance/#Direct%20Operations

Your priority is to surface topics that create:
- Urgency  
- Relevance  
- Exclusivity  
- Authority  
- Meaningful consequences for agents  

---

# Returning Topic Options  
After research, you must present **exactly five strong topic options**.

Each option must:
- Be concise  
- Include a strong potential hook  
- State why agents should care  
- Tie directly to closings, timelines, risk, or client experience  
- Be something a title rep can credibly talk about  

If the user says **“Search again,”** you must redo the research and return five new topics.

Once the user chooses a topic, you must begin scriptwriting.

---

# Scriptwriting Rules

## Length  
Write approximately **120–160 words**, equal to **45–60 seconds spoken**.

## Tone & Personality  
You must:
- Match the user’s selected personality/style  
- Write conversationally and confidently  
- Use contractions and short sentences  
- Avoid filler, hype, clichés, buzzwords, or vague generalities  
- Speak directly to **real estate agents**, never consumers  
- Focus on implications for deals, client prep, communication, and timelines  

## Hooks  
You must begin every script with a strong, agent-centered hook that:
- Creates urgency or curiosity  
- Addresses a real risk or timeline issue  
- Promises fast payoff  

You must **not** use generic openers like:  
“Today I want to talk about title insurance.”

## Agent Value Requirements  
You must deliver one or more of the following:
- A risk agents underestimate  
- A pattern you see across many files  
- A shift affecting closings  
- A practical step agents should take  
- Something that protects clients or prevents delays  

## Credibility Requirements  
You must:
- Position the speaker as a knowledgeable title professional  
- Avoid legal advice  
- Avoid guaranteeing underwriting outcomes  
- Ensure all information is accurate, safe, and non-speculative  

## CTA Rules  
If the user requested a CTA, you must include it at the end in one clean, natural sentence.  
If they said “no CTA,” you must not include one.

## Delivery Format  
You must return the final script inside **one single preformatted text block** (triple-backtick fenced) with **no commentary**.

Example:
```
[script only]
```

## Teleprompter Optimization  
You must:
- Write with natural spoken rhythm  
- Avoid long paragraphs  
- Avoid stage directions unless requested  
- Write the script as if the speaker is talking directly to camera  

---

# Chain of Verification  
Before delivering the script, you must run an internal self-check (not shown to the user).  
If anything fails verification, you must correct it before returning the final script.

You must verify:

### 1. Topic Alignment  
- Does the script match the chosen topic or data?  
- Is it relevant to real est

                                            Prompts
                    
                    Agent Appointment Intelligence Dossier

                    
                        January 24, 2026                    
                
            
        

        
            
                


What This Is


This resource gives you a complete workflow for researching a real estate agent before a meeting. You’ll use the deep-research tool of your preferred LLM (e.g. Gemini, Perplexity, ChatGPT, et al.) to generate a detailed “agent dossier,” then feed that dossier into NotebookLM to produce a structured audio overview (like a podcast) you can listen to before an appointment, call, or first contact.



The workflow is designed for vendors, service partners, and business-development professionals who want to understand:




Who the agent is (professionally and personally, based on public info),



How they work and make decisions,



Where they might need support,



How to approach them with the right message and tone.








How To Use This



Copy and Run the Full Research PromptScroll down to PROMPT 1 and copy it. Open your chosen LLM (Perplexity, Gemini, Grok, or ChatGPT), in the message box, enable its deep-research mode, paste the prompt in. Before submitting the prompt, replace the placeholders for the agent’s name, market/city, and brokerage, then submit it to generate the full agent dossier in a markdown format.



Copy the LLM Output (the “Dossier”) When the model finishes its response, copy the entire output it produces (all sections, top to bottom). Tip: Look for the icon of two overlapping, rounded-edge squares to copy the full response.



Open NotebookLM Go to https://notebooklm.google.com and sign in with your Google account.



Create a New Notebook Create a notebook dedicated to this agent (for example: “Agent Dossier – [Agent Name]”).



Add the Dossier as a Source In NotebookLM, click Add Source → Copied text. Paste the full dossier you copied from the LLM’s response and save it as a source.



Add Any Extra Sources (Optional) If you have them, add additional sources as separate items, such as:Crystal Knows profile, transaction or production reports, public bios or articles, any other relevant text documents.NotebookLM will use all of these together when generating the audio overview.



Generate the Audio Overview In the right-hand sidebar of the notebook—after you review the next step—click Audio Overview to create a spoken briefing based on your sources. 



Customize the Audio Overview

Click the pencil icon (edit) on the Audio Overview panel.



Choose a Deep Dive style (or equivalent long-form option).



Paste the short ordering prompt from PROMPT 2 into the instructions box.



Save and generate the Audio Overview.





Listen or Download Play the Audio Overview directly inside NotebookLM, or download it so you can listen on the way to your meeting or before sending outreach.




PROMPT 1 — Full Agent Research Prompt




	
		Prompt
		
			
				
				
					
				

				
				
					
				

				Copy
			
		
	

	
		
			

## Role
You are a **Strategic Intelligence Analyst for a Title Company** tasked with preparing a comprehensive relationship and business-development briefing on a Real Estate Agent.

Your job is to research the agent’s professional footprint, public presence, and operating style to help a Title Sales Executive build rapport, time outreach correctly, and position the title company as a long-term partner.

—

## Custom Inputs
– **Agent Name**: [AGENT NAME]  
– **Market/City**: [CITY / MARKET]  
– **Brokerage**: [BROKERAGE]

—

## Research Scope

### 1. Professional Profile
– Brokerage and team affiliation  
– Years in business  
– Primary markets served  
– Buyer vs seller mix (if evident)  
– Production indicators (reviews, transaction counts, team size, rankings)  
– Specialties (luxury, relocation, investors, new construction, distressed, etc.)

---

### 2. Transaction Behavior Signals
– Typical price bands operated in  
– New construction vs resale mix  
– Investor vs retail client mix  
– Volume vs boutique service positioning  
– Frequency of fast-close or multi-offer scenarios  

---

### 3. Digital & Brand Presence
Research and summarize insights from:
– Zillow  
– Realtor.com  
– LinkedIn  
– Facebook  
– X (Twitter)  
– Instagram / TikTok (if public)  
– Personal or team website  
– Google results and public articles  

Include:
– Brand tone and positioning  
– Posting frequency  
– Topics emphasized  
– Messaging themes (education, lifestyle, speed, trust, volume, etc.)  
– Visual style and professionalism  

---

### 4. Awards & Credentials
– Designations (CRS, GRI, ABR, SFR, CDPE, etc.)  
– Brokerage or industry awards  
– Leadership roles  
– Teaching, coaching, or speaking involvement  

---

### 5. Personal & Lifestyle Signals (public only)
– Hobbies or interests mentioned  
– Community involvement  
– Family mentions (high level only)  
– Causes, charities, or events supported  
– Personality style inferred from content (analytical, relational, energetic, structured, etc.)

---

### 6. Process & Pain-Point Clues
– Evidence of centralized vs decentralized transaction coordination  
– Mentions of timelines, stress-free closings, or process  
– Public frustrations or problem themes (lenders, inspections, delays, appraisals, etc.)  
– Complexity of deals handled (relocations, estates, foreclosures, multi-state, etc.)

---

### 7. Decision-Making Style
– Data-driven vs relationship-driven  
– Systems-focused vs personality-led  
– Brand-first vs volume-first  
– Leader, rainmaker, or hybrid role  

---

### 8. Timing & Trigger Events
– Recent brokerage changes  
– Team expansion or recruiting signals  
– Major awards or rankings  
– Market expansion  
– Public growth language (“we’re growing,” “welcome to the team,” etc.)

---

### 9. Vendor & Partner Loyalty Signals
– Mentions or tags of title companies or escrow partners  
– Patterns of repeated vendor use  
– Language indicating exclusive or preferred partners  
– Co-branded content with service providers  

---

### 10. Compliance & Risk Sensitivity (public only)
– Emphasis on disclosures, ethics, or consumer education  
– Any public litigation, complaints, or regulatory issues  
– Tone around protection, transparency, or caution  

---

### 11. Influence & Relationship Map
– Who appears to control vendor decisions (agent, team lead, TC, ops)  
– Senior or anchor agents on team  
– Visibility of operations or admin staff  

—

## Output Format (Markdown)

### Agent Overview
– Name:  
– Brokerage:  
– Market:  
– Years Active:  
– Primary Focus:  

---

### Business Model & Production
– Team or solo:  
– Production indicators:  
– Client type mix:  
– Price band tendencies:  

---

### Brand & Online Presence
– Zillow/Realtor.com positioning:  
– Social media themes:  
– Website messaging:  
– Brand personality:  

---

### Awards & Credentials
– Designations:  
– Awards:  
– Leadership roles:  

---

### Personal & Lifestyle Insights
– Interests:  
– Community involvement:  
– Personality style:  

---

### Process & Operational Signals
– Transaction coordination style:  
– Complexity of deals:  
– Public pain-point clues:  

---

### Decision & Growth Signals
– Decision-making style:  
– Growth trajectory:  
– Trigger events:  

---

### Partner & Loyalty Indicators
– Current partner signals:  
– Openness to new partners:  

---

### Conversation Starters
Provide:
– 3 professional talking points  
– 3 personal rapport builders  
– 2 value-add angles for a title partner  

---

### Executive Summary
– 3 personalization strategies  
– 3 strategic notes for relationship building  
– 1 recommended outreach angle (warm, consultative, or strategic)

—

## Important Instructions
– Use only publicly available information  
– No speculation or invasive details  
– Clearly note when data is unavailable  
– Write in professional, client-ready Markdown  
– Prioritize insights useful for relationship building and business development  
– Cite sources when possible

		
		
			
				
			
			Show more
		
	





PROMPT 2 — Audio Ordering Prompt




	
		Prompt
		
			
				
				
					
				

				
				
					
				

				Copy
			
		
	

	
		
			

Provide a thorough briefing for a potential business partner preparing to meet this real estate agent. Start with who they are (market, brokerage, years active, team/solo, production). Then outline their brand tone, online presence, and personal signals they’ve p

TITLE: AI Walkthrough Workflow – System Setup

                                            Guides
                    
                    AI Walkthrough Workflow – System Setup

                    
                        January 20, 2026                    
                
            
        

        
            
                


What This Is


This guide walks you through producing a fully AI-generated property walkthrough video using a still listing photo. You’ll create smooth camera motion from a single image, generate a timed voiceover that matches the flow of your footage, and finish by adding a talking-head avatar overlay for a polished, agent-led listing tour.







How to Use This


Follow the steps in order. Each step builds the final video.



1. Create Walkthrough Footage (AI-Generated Camera Motion)




Gather high-quality listing photos for every room you want to feature.

If it’s not your listing, obtain required consent.



Do not materially alter the environment. AI must not add or remove architectural elements such as windows, doors, closets, railings, openings, or light sources. Always doublecheck before you publish your video(s). 





Open a generative video tool.

Primary option: Elevenlabs.io → Playground → Image & Video.



Alternatives: Artlist, Higgsfield, or Luma AI.



You need access to video models such as Kling O1, which (presently), handles motion-based instructions well.





In Elevenlabs:

Navigate to Playground → Image & Video.



In the model picker (lower left of the prompt modal), select Kling O1.



Set Aspect Ratio: 9:16.



Set Duration: 5 seconds



Set Generations: 1 to conserve tokens.



At the top, click Image Refs, then upload the reference photo for the room.





Paste the following prompt in the prompt box (modify if desired):




Generate a video walkthrough based strictly on the provided reference photo. Use a realistic filming style—such as a handheld gimbal, slow push-in, or subtle glide—while keeping all motion physically plausible and limited to what the image shows. Preserve every object, color, material, and lighting detail exactly as it appears in the reference image, and do not add, remove, or alter anything. Do not invent or imply any unseen elements, including windows, doors, railings, openings, adjacent spaces, or light sources. If something isn’t clearly visible in the photo, it must not appear in the video. The camera should move smoothly and calmly, staying fully anchored to the visual information in the reference image.




Press Generate (upper right corner).

Your clip will appear in the History tab, where you can download it.





Repeat the process for every room.

Produce one 5-second clip per image.



Download all clips to your device.



Time saver: You can have multiple generations running at the same time versus waiting for each video to finish one at a time. 





Open your video editor (CapCut, Descript, et al.).

Create a new video project



Drag clips into your timeline in the order you want.



Add transitions if you want smooth room-to-room flow.



Export the assembled walkthrough video.






2. Generate a Timed Voiceover (Claude + Elevenlabs)




Visit the Time-Synced Property Tour Voiceover Script Generator resource inside AiM: 



Paste the provided prompt into Claude.

Once you submit your prompt, it’ll ask you questions.



First, upload your exported walkthrough video.



Claude will read the footage, identify each room’s timing, and write a voiceover that matches the exact sequence and duration.



It will format the script with embedded XML cues for tone, pacing, and emphasis.





Once satisfied with the script, open Elevenlabs → Text to Speech.

Paste the script.



Choose your voice clone.



Generate the audio and download the MP3.






3. Add Your Avatar Overlay (HeyGen + CapCut)




Go to HeyGen → Avatars (left sidebar).

If you don’t have an avatar yet, create one.



If you do, hover over it and click the Create video with this avatar button.





Do not paste your script into HeyGen.

Even with an Elevenlabs voice integration connection, HeyGen doesn’t interpret XML, which causes flat delivery.





Instead:

Tap the Audio button just below where you’d type your script.



Upload the MP3 you created in Elevenlabs.



Use a 16:9 avatar style so your body doesn’t clip at the edges.



Tap Generate and download your avatar video.





Open CapCut (or your editor) again.

Drag the avatar video onto a new track above your walkthrough footage.



Select the avatar clip → navigate to Video in the project panel and find the tab labeled Remove BG → enable Auto Removal (paid feature).





Resize and position the avatar.

With your avatar clip selected in the timeline, in the player panel, click to select and move and/or resize your avatar



Place it smaller in the lower-right quadrant to keep the property front-and-center and minimize any realism inconsistencies.





Export your final video.










From the Session: This guide was introduced in AI Walkthrough Workflow.

                                            Guides
                    
                    26 AI Tools Heading Into 2026

                    
                        December 31, 2025                    
                
            
        

        
            
                


One of the most common questions I get is some version of “What AI tools do you use or recommend?”



This list is my answer—26 tools I either rely on daily or actively test. The first section covers my core stack: the tools that power my workflow. The second section highlights tools worth experimenting with, depending on what you’re working on.



Use this as a starting point. You don’t need all of these—nobody does. But knowing what’s out there (and what each tool does well) helps you make smarter choices about where to invest your time and money.



Core Stack (Tools I Rely On)



1. ChatGPT – Still my default for thinking things through, writing, voice dictation, and everyday problem-solving. Projects and custom GPTs make it far more useful and nuanced than a blank chat window. Plus its built-in App Store to connect tools like Mailchimp and Adobe.



2. Claude – When I’m working with data, creating files like docs or spreadsheets, or writing code (e.g., HTML for email campaigns), this is where I go to get it done intelligently.



3. Gemini – Nano Banana Pro for image and graphic design, plus Veo 3.1 for short video generation, are top-tier. It rivals ChatGPT for general use and is built into Google’s ecosystem of apps.



4. Perplexity – My go-to for deep research. And its Tasks feature lets you run recurring research automatically—similar to Google Alerts, but smarter.



5. Canva – Still the fastest way to produce real design assets like slides or social graphics. Its AI editing capabilities are super solid.



6. CapCut – The video editor I use most often. It adds (useful) AI assistance without taking control away from you.



7. Captions – Especially strong for subtitles and AI-assisted edits. I use it alongside CapCut, not instead of it.



8. Comet – Perplexity’s agentic browser. “Agentic” here means you can give it instructions and it will actually control the browser for you—clicking, navigating, and completing tasks on your behalf.



9. n8n – Not beginner-friendly, but extremely powerful. This is how you build real automation inside your business—connecting systems and bringing your marketing and operations to life without manual work.



10. Zapier Agents – Much easier than n8n. You give an agent clear instructions (a prompt), and it handles the steps across your apps without you building every step manually. Copilot (built in) can configure the agent for you.



11. NotebookLM – Excellent for learning. You feed it your sources (links or pasted text), and it stays grounded in that material while creating summaries you can chat with, podcast-style audio overviews, infographics, or reports.



12. ElevenLabs – The best voice cloning I’ve used. The “Pro” clone is worth it if you’re podcasting or recording voiceovers for videos.



13. HeyGen – The closest thing I’ve seen to a usable video avatar of yourself, especially when paired with audio from ElevenLabs.



Worth Testing (Useful, Not Essential)



14. Otterly.ai – Helps you monitor and improve how you appear inside AI-driven search and answer engines.



15. ManyChat – Still the best tool for Instagram DMs. The addition of AI lets conversations go deeper and feel more natural, instead of being limited to prewritten scripts.



16. Descript – A strong video editor with the best transcription-based editing I’ve seen. You can edit video by editing the text.



17. Sora – OpenAI’s video-focused social platform. Early and imperfect, but interesting for experimenting with short-form video.



18. Atlas – OpenAI’s contender in agentic browsing. Chromium-based, but not as capable as Comet in my experience.



19. Gamma – A fast way to create slide presentations, though I still default to Canva or Gemini for most production work.



20. Lovable – Part of the “vibe coding” category. You describe what you want, and it builds websites or simple apps without needing to code.



21. LumaLabs – Quality video generation, but what really stands out is Interactive Scenes, which let you turn a video you record (like walking around a home) into an explorable 3D model you can embed or export.



22. Higgsfield – A creator-friendly video platform with guided options and presets for trending social videos, instead of starting from a blank prompt.



23. RunwayML – Known for realistic video generation and the ability to keep the same character consistent across multiple scenes.



24. PLAUD – A small physical recorder you bring to meetings. It records conversations and uses AI afterward to summarize, analyze, and help you remember what was said and promised.



25. Artlist.io – Longtime source for music and b-roll, now also lets you generate video using top AI models like Veo, Sora, and Kling.



26. Wisprflow – A dictation app that lets you speak out loud and have your words appear directly inside any app on your computer.



Want More?



Check out the Tools to Test section on your main profile page. I keep an updated list there of AI tools organized by use case—so you can quickly find what you need based on what you’re trying to accomplish.





Related Topics:



Smart, Savvy AI Spending

                                            Bots
                    
                    Nano Banana Pro Instagram Reel Cover Photo Generator

                    
                        December 10, 2025        
        

        
            
                


What This Is


This is a Gemini Gem—essentially a specialized chat inside your Gemini account that always starts with the same underlying system instructions. It works much like a custom GPT inside ChatGPT. You load your brand rules—colors, fonts, layout preferences, and workflow details—into the Gem’s Instructions field. Gemini treats those instructions as the permanent foundation for every new conversation you have with that Gem.



That’s the benefit: every time you start a new chat with this Gem, you don’t have to re-explain your brand or the process. The Gem already knows the rules and follows them consistently, making the entire workflow repeatable.



This particular Gem uses Nano Banana Pro, the newest and most precise image model inside Gemini. Nano Banana Pro isn’t just for generating images—it can remove backgrounds cleanly, modify or augment images, and produce real graphic design outputs with strong accuracy. That precision makes it ideal for Instagram Reel cover photos, which need three curiosity drivers:




a compelling designed backdrop,



a frame of you that reads at a glance,



and clear text that draws people in.




Cover photos won’t boost views on a single Reel, but they do increase views across your entire profile. Most Reel views come from non-followers. When they tap into your profile, strong covers help them quickly decide what else to watch. This Gem automates that entire design process for you.







How To Use This



Go to gemini.google.com, and confirm you’re logged into the Google account tied to your Gemini access.



A paid plan isn’t required, though Nano Banana Pro performs more reliably with upgraded access.



Open the left sidebar (the three-line “hamburger” icon).



Click Gems to open your Gem Manager.



Tap + New Gem, then give it any name and description you like.



Locate the Instructions text box. This is where you’ll copy and paste the full prompt below—but don’t save yet, because you’ll need to make the changes outlined in the next step first.



Before you save the Gem, you’ll need to update a few brand-specific sections inside the prompt. These are the only parts you should change. Everything else must stay exactly as written so the Gem functions correctly. You’re customizing the prompt so Nano Banana Pro knows your brand colors, fonts, visual style, and the type of background it should design for your cover photos. The sections you’ll update are shown below:





	
		Prompt
		
			
				
				
					
				

				
				
					
				

				Copy
			
		
	

	
		
			

[COLOR_PALETTE]
- Primary:
- Secondary:
- Accent:

[TYPOGRAPHY]
- Heading font:
- Body font:

[VISUAL_IDENTITY / MOTIFS]
- Motifs or recurring elements:
- Texture or design preferences:

[BACKGROUND_STYLE]
- Preferred aesthetic or style direction:

		
		
			
				
			
			Show more
		
	






Once you’ve made those customizations, simply save the Gem. Everything else is turnkey.



To generate a new cover photo:

Start a new chat with your Gem.



Upload one frame from your talking-head video.



Provide the text you want on the cover.



If you want an image blended into the background or added in the foreground, just tell the Gem—it’s already prepared for that.



The Gem removes your background (from the video frame) and blends everything into a finished, on-brand design automatically. 





If you want to remove the small Gemini watermark:

Open Canva and upload the image your Gem produced.



Place it on/in a 9:16 canvas.



Select the image and tap Edit in the top toolbar.



Under Magic Studio (in the lefthand modal that popped up), choose Magic Eraser.



In the “Select area to erase” mode, choose “Click,” and tap cursor over the Gemini logo.



Then select Erase to remove it.



Click Share in the top-right corner.



Set the file type to PNG.



Download your finished cover photo.







	
		Prompt
		
			
				
				
					
				

				
				
					
				

				Copy
			
		
	

	
		
			

You are an expert Social Media Graphic Designer. Your role is to generate high-conversion Instagram Reel Covers (9:16 aspect ratio) by compositing a user-provided video frame with specific brand elements.

### 1. BRAND VARIABLES
Strictly adhere to these settings for every generation.

**A. Color Palette**
* Primary: [PRIMARY BRAND COLOR — e.g., HEX, RGB, OR “BLUE”]
* Secondary: [SECONDARY BRAND COLOR — SUPPORTING SHADE]
* Tertiary: [OPTIONAL ACCENT COLOR — USED SPARINGLY]
* Background Base: [BASE BACKGROUND COLOR — DARK OR LIGHT]
* Text Color: [HIGH-CONTRAST TEXT COLOR]
* Neutral: [NEUTRAL GRAY OR MUTED TONE]

**B. Typography Style**
* Headline Font: [HEADLINE FONT OR STYLE TO EMULATE — E.G., “BOLD MODERN SANS-SERIF”]
* Body Font: [BODY FONT OR STYLE — E.G., “LIGHT CLEAN SANS-SERIF”]

**C. Visual Identity**
* Motifs: [VISUAL MOTIFS — E.G., GEOMETRIC LINES, BRUSH STROKES, TECH ELEMENTS, SHAPES]
* Background Preference: [BACKGROUND STYLE — E.G., “GRADIENT FROM PRIMARY→SECONDARY,” “TEXTURED WASH,” “MINIMAL FLAT COLOR”]

### 2. GENERATION WORKFLOW

**Step 1: Background Layer**
* Create a 9:16 vertical canvas.
* Apply a gradient defined in Background Preference.
* Overlay Motifs subtly at low opacity.

**Step 2: Subject Layer**
* Identify the talking-head subject in the uploaded frame.
* Remove the original background completely.
* Position the subject centrally; face must remain within the middle 50% of the canvas height.
* Do not alter physical traits.
* Optional: Apply lighting adjustments or a duotone effect using Primary and Secondary colors.

**Step 3: Typography Layer**
* Render the user’s provided text using the Typography Style.
* Headlines should be large and bold; secondary text smaller and lighter.
* Do not place text in the bottom 20% or at the extreme top.
* Ensure text and the subject’s face remain visible when cropped to 1:1.
* Use Text Color; add drop shadow if necessary.

**Step 4: Additional Imagery Scan**
* Analyze the user’s prompt for added elements.
* Background elements → integrate into Step 1 as subtle texture.
* Foreground props → integrate into Step 2 as physical objects near the subject.

### 3. OPERATIONAL RULES
* If an image is provided without text, ask for the text.
* Always output a 9:16 vertical Instagram Reel cover.
* If generated text contains spelling errors, apologize and regenerate.

		
		
			
				
			
			Show more
